North is up and east is left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig1.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/48563926/stw2924fig1.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig1.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"48563926\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/stw2924fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&Signature=1npw76UH-KQe3QDh7ECLOwDm3IFT5LqkbIu2QvRhWDfpOGuLWVSBAfvE04ICpDOFNY3Af2ik-cSPUlqB4oys2h6IBaO5XMZgYk92ftC70grcLaYKHwlzOtPzEGrRDc5WhIwM1EWfE-fmF8H23WmXklwpPnWKWAgbauF3pmt7ApGykJC4kdSarIbyZ9M0IoKonKoBo8p2hC~XzJZvz-gBo4L6wpXBihXC3Jp0pOG8PY162UAQkGAZx7ZJQsUPZShBZ96ycVQVkTsEaPG-VBgjipESDfd2kZK98CaruxD-V3EiftJj0xGKSXflm-bH~9ycKuF1970kpDEMnKDAZaVO0A__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=48563926&ar=2525975&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig1.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Colour (<em>gri</em>) composite of HSC J1152+0047 showing the four blue lensed images (A, B, C and D) in an <em>Einstein</em>-cross configuration. Apart from the central lensing galaxy (G), a companion galaxy (G1, close to image C) is probably contributing to lensing. The image is 10\u00a0arcsec on the side. North is up and east is left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We measured the relative positions and photometry of the lens galaxies and the lensed images from the HSC imaging using <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> (Peng et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>). In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>, we show all of the HSC bands and the model-subtracted residual images for each band, respectively. The seeing in the HSC-<em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, <em>i</em>, <em>z</em> and <em>Y</em> images is found to be 0.55, 0.46, 0.45, 0.60 and 0.61\u00a0arcsec, respectively, as per hscPipe. All of the four lensed images are fitted with a point spread function (PSF) model in all bands except in the <em>i</em> band, where a S\u00e9rsic profile is better fit to images A and B. The lens galaxy G is fitted with a double S\u00e9rsic model and the companion galaxy G1 is fitted with a PSF model. For lens galaxy G, we used the <em>z</em>-band best-fitting model as prior when fitting the double S\u00e9rsic model in other bands. The relative positions from <em>z</em> band and photometry in all bands along with errors from <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> are given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. We note that the colours (<em>g</em> \u2212 <em>r</em> and <em>r</em> \u2212 <em>i</em>) of the lensed images (see Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>) are consistent with the colours of a quasar at <em>z</em> \u223c 4 (Richards et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"48563928\" scrollto-destination=\"48563928\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/m_stw2924fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&amp;Signature=ebOMuvqArejvFylNc5YvZuer3rqQbCADIJKyjec9DX8IXiG5B8r1FpuYjEpGO7WbqnQM0zKFufTECMKeeN7G2~24Mk-gq6bYOF7yK9K-3wdU15NQgJGy1LnUsXeJD6xYXu6T3fJs1fy1Gvjnji9X9E4ywv90fG7j1Z848U4IYZO9CYXuW~RI-xJ8fK6hUI9Wcu~2tZPCAf1f-UCmZqkaukZKeYXXNXsVKV6I3df95o1l5-As2AEflWv7PoWfejt9YoLyO5E7DH0cYRmF5YGe~hr1zi7Z7IsYq1qckLcjM8HU1g8CUdVd6-VcDtwPp9l7uxrcJOZl3KpDJYdCGMCqng__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"galfit modelling results in HSC grizY bands. 0.79271) deg. The relative astrometry is from the <em>z</em> band in units of arcsec. The positive directions of \u0394<em>x</em> and \u0394<em>y</em> are north and west, respectively. For every object, the second row shows the errors from <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>. Except the <em>i</em> band for images A and B where a S\u00e9rsic model is chosen, all of the lensed images are fitted with a PSF model. The magnitudes are corrected for Galactic extinction.</p></span></div></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/48563929\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We find that our system is detected in the near-infrared (NIR) imaging taken by the VISTA Kilo-degree Infrared Galaxy survey (VIKING; Edge et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). The <em>JHK</em>s imaging obtained from the VISTA Science Archive (VSA)<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. The brighter pair of lensed images shows hints of presence in the <em>J</em> and <em>H</em> imaging, whereas they are well detected in the <em>Ks</em> band. We fit a PSF model to the brighter pair of images and a S\u00e9rsic model to the galaxy G with <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>. We use the Two Micron All Sky Survey (2MASS) <em>K</em>-band magnitude of a nearby star for the flux calibration of our VIKING <em>Ks</em>-band photometry. The images A, B and galaxy G have <em>Ks</em>-band magnitudes of 19.31 \u00b1 0.10, 20.28 \u00b1 0.27 and 15.23 \u00b1 0.14 Vega mag, respectively, where the reported errors are from <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>.</p>                        <a id=\"48563931\" scrollto-destination=\"48563931\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/m_stw2924fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&amp;Signature=zo656abeTdWHt5S9YkEi0gJX5G7aAi9lPz8zDGtsNKCAdSE3SuexgpbJcK28boiY8cDGi7Qh6Dku~PpurAjQNMAKkepG748B-Ir~WZwv~PRelJnnpmDxva83P0W-BpGfy1rHUtje2dkMWM6QMNAOMc952xUn8T3YSbPMsKtVjONWRK2eAotZeEDHSDag9RLP2zQeZ0w68GFJp6hVcyuJTpgCd0oQJ~UM2qdF2JAMIjGp2OP9GOR9c5BbCO~tUMtUft1Q7Y3ndQzBw~c8Vmno7u91wsQQbG81OpAVZP-vQUuZ-Z4oo-yLhWtmOl9riw1Xz4RDW~RUi8c36PGCaWrSCw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"From left to right, NIR VIKING imaging in filters (limiting AB magnitudes) J(22.1), H(21.5), and Ks(21.2) followed by WISE imaging in bands W1 and W2. The brighter pair of lensed images are close to the detection limits of VIKING <em>J</em> and <em>H</em> imaging, whereas they are detected in the <em>Ks</em> band. <em>W</em>1 and <em>W</em>2 imaging shows emission from the lens galaxy alone (see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>). The two green circles mark the location of sources detected from the <em>WISE</em> catalogues. North is up and east is left. The bar shows a scale of 9\u00a0arcsec.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We also checked if the lensed source is detected in the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em>) imaging (Wright et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). While <em>W</em>1 and <em>W</em>2 bands show emission at the centre of HSC J1152+0047, we cannot confirm the presence of the lensed source due to source confusion owing to the low resolution (6\u00a0arcsec). The source catalogue from <em>WISE</em> reports two detections. One of the sources coincides with the lens galaxy G, whereas the second source does not coincide with any of the lensed images or galaxy G1 (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>). The lens galaxy G has <em>W</em>1 = 15.18 \u00b1 0.04 and <em>W</em>2 = 15.04 \u00b1 0.08 Vega mag, respectively. In bands <em>W</em>3 and <em>W</em>4, there is no detection of any emission from either the lens or the lensed source, thus providing a strong upper limit on source flux. The sensitivities with a signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) of 5 for <em>W</em>3 and <em>W</em>4 bands are 11.5 and 7.87 Vega mag, respectively.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We show the flux ratios of images B, C, D with respect to image A in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> for all of the HSC <em>grizY</em> bands and VIKING <em>Ks</em> band. All of the three flux ratios appear nearly uniform across the optical bands with no strong evidence for differential reddening. However, the flux ratio of the brighter image pair (B/A \u223c 0.41 \u00b1 0.11) in <em>Ks</em> band (observed in year 2011) is discrepant with the flux ratio of 0.95 \u00b1 0.02 seen in the HSC data (observed in year 2015). In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec6-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec6-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">6.2</a>, we discuss various phenomena that are known to introduce chromatic (i.e. wavelength-dependent) variation in the flux ratios of lensed images in the attempt to find plausible explanation for this discrepancy.</p>                        <a id=\"48563934\" scrollto-destination=\"48563934\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/m_stw2924fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&amp;Signature=u6KfbHLyNWLgT2BnBGwyDwbDzn8EBF3LkR8YnaeIYP7fMwgpwMUuJ6TdqsTeuEee1liDYKdvRR02yYUY1Fz-DtjtGWunpoFbcBbqWQZ9BnBZq3aymTfQV6B8NMhTuUrjFuef7BlGCaN~jTwC4LzXOkGs2xRpAcE72Ru0D0c0lhI5QP1xAYxnWVDv9unMzrBqhziPKLFK703PcAlBzcLcBnNPeeGaknlnnjA3kIEGajdeT~SMQJDEAP09hFvpiqsG~eS3CvqFSQqeNesW-yglVWjb7oj12PbDyshx6uiMTxde9lyvxPdn13bT1DMmNg~v9xwW0H9gZ2r16SSkpFYLkQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Flux ratios of the lensed images as a function of wavelengths in the optical (HSC grizY band; see also Table\u00a01). For the brighter pair of images, we also include the Ks-band data in the NIR.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig4.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/48563934/stw2924fig4.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig4.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"48563934\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/stw2924fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&Signature=IVgmuhYiJQaXrnw5J2-2SQFXJX5JrukVnLqUd9JJKrvd9bfa3vL-Z~mEGGj8RhPDoy36525YypepmMyZoi-5-iInqP1r5iWd9w0QKlI-ezNJDAwoKDe~Qyx8s741c6uG6MT0y4iVEdkbmlWqMzl-DlYg8FHxp-6i8s5Nx6n7LdaUr215CyoTbOU5-XpeyoDPB1rp35T8BQ6t~DCXj~X7CRWz9qXckAgBz-8m7fXTCboadB7bGRvytQO3xplQ-3y1Z15vewbnGFZTo9sNx9Bh-KECUV-k8LmSPUVWW255KsrmxB35HtVldcJUs6Og3bp7qqza23U2QmFs4RExsLiYcA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=48563934&ar=2525975&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stw2924fig4.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Flux ratios of the lensed images as a function of wavelengths in the optical (HSC <em>grizY</em> band; see also Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). For the brighter pair of images, we also include the <em>Ks</em>-band data in the NIR.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Images C and D are detected in the optical bands only. The flux of image C is likely contaminated by the emission from satellite galaxy (G1) in the reddest (<em>Y</em>) band where G1 becomes more prominent (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> and Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). In the bluer bands, the fluxes of images C and D are comparable as expected for this image configuration from lensing. Although the presence of G1 could affect the magnification of image C, we do not detect any significant difference between relative fluxes of C and D.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563936 id=\"48563936\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 SPECTROSCOPIC OBSERVATION OF HSC J1152+0047</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We obtained spectra of HSC J1152+0047 on 2016 March 11 (Program ID: GN-2016A-FT-7) with Gemini North Telescope through the Fast Turnaround programme (Mason et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) with seeing around 0.6\u20130.7\u00a0arcsec. We used <span class=\"small-caps\">gmos</span> in the long-slit mode with R400-G5305 grating and GG455 blocking filter. The width of the slit was set to 1.0\u00a0arcsec. This provides a spectral resolution <em>R</em>\u00a0=\u00a01918 and wavelength coverage from 4000 to 8000 \u00c5. Two perpendicular slit configurations were used to acquire spectra of all four lensed images, as well as the lens galaxy G. The brighter pair was observed for 15\u00a0min and the fainter pair was observed for 45\u00a0min. The companion galaxy G1 is aligned with the fainter pair and fell on the second slit but was too faint to be detected.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We carried out the data reduction using <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span> with the dedicated <span class=\"small-caps\">gmos</span> package (v1.13). The spectra were bias-subtracted, flat-fielded and sky-subtracted. We used CuAr lamp to calibrate the wavelength. Because the lensed image spectra are very faint and close to the spectra of the lens galaxy (&lt;2\u00a0arcsec for the brighter pair and &lt;1\u00a0arcsec for the fainter pair), we manually select the aperture size and spectra position to extract the lensed image spectra. This is done using the \u2018gsextract\u2019 routine in an interactive mode. For the fainter pair, the spectra are dominated by the lens galaxy light. To avoid contamination from the lens for the fainter pair of images, we scaled the red part (&gt;7000 \u00c5) of the lens spectrum to match the spectrum of the fainter images assuming that most of the redder part is dominated by the emission from the lens. We then subtracted this normalized lens galaxy spectrum from the spectra of the fainter images. We calibrate the flux based on spectroscopic standard star. We estimate flux errors using the variance at each pixel, which is calculated from the raw image, and taking into account the effects of each reduction step using error propagation, till extracting the one-dimensional spectra.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The spectra of the lens galaxy and the four lensed images are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>. The spectra of each of the lensed images in the figure are smoothed with a Gaussian filter of 2.7 \u00c5 and vertical offsets are added for illustrative purpose. The redshift of the lens galaxy is found to be <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> = 0.466 \u00b1 0.001 through the identification of Ca\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> H and K absorption lines near the 4000 \u00c5 break as expected for a typical early-type galaxy. As mentioned earlier, we do not have a spectrum of G1 but our photometric redshift estimate (<em>z</em><sub><em>l</em>G1</sub> = 0.46 \u00b1 0.15) suggests that G1 is a gravitationally bound satellite of galaxy G. The similarity in the spectra of the four images confirms the strong lensing nature of this system. The redshift of the lensed source is found to be <em>z</em><sub>s</sub> = 3.76 \u00b1 0.01 based on the identification of the Ly-\u03b1 emission line. Also, the stacked spectrum shows other high ionization state lines, e.g. NV in emission and C\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> in absorption which are rather weak but their presence is consistent with the interpretation of the stronger emission line as Ly\u00a0\u03b1 (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"48563940\" scrollto-destination=\"48563940\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/m_stw2924fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&amp;Signature=Eeifx8IVG8mFRCuZkNx094k2YMNjlwubBFXoI-ROpRcfpdXas9dnFdcz9lUpkiCFKMwjngiAt2nQIDgmhBKwJFCq1hqsAAlH3W5UMgXAq7Bip-3ix9a2f7MMjRNSLcsMoIZs1P~5Mx-yMS15fXjdf90CwWdHymq3DSMdeBYMOiNQoZv7Mbx2QGkEV2h0wZbKGCMFGhzMrAd80jVXxNkMdGt4WS0wh4qWlPWf-AYi~g3jAZlNISADLPhzkJWQeEvnJggN9FU36rIPdD3NNgTx98PxvcmCPPWVWVIBBVtewCzoJexMyO9wEDn1J5MF7L8HDTQEL7S9mFxhWEwYK92esQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Upper left: the spectrum of the lensing galaxy from our Gemini gmos spectroscopic follow-up observation. Lower: the stacked spectrum of the lensed source with commonly found emission and absorption lines labelled. The error on the spectrum is shown with the shaded region (grey). The two vertical bars (grey) shown in all panels indicate absorption features probably due to telluric contamination.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563941 id=\"48563941\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 LENS MASS MODELLING</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We used the lens modelling software <span class=\"small-caps\">glafic</span> (Oguri\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) for lens mass modelling. We started with <em>i</em>-band HSC image where information from each pixel offers data constraints for the mass models.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens galaxies are each modelled with an isothermal density profile. Galaxy G is allowed to have ellipticity whereas galaxy G1 is assumed to have spherically symmetric mass distribution since G1 is less massive and the contribution from the quadrupole moment of its mass distribution is not expected to be significant. While we allowed for external shear to be present earlier in our models, we found that this was highly degenerate with the ellipticity of G and did not improve the fit significantly. Hence, we removed it for simplicity. Thus, our final lens mass model consists of an isothermal ellipsoid (SIE) for G and an isothermal sphere (SIS) for G1.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We tested different assumptions when modelling the light profile of the lensed source. Our list of models comprised (i) single PSF, (ii) single S\u00e9rsic and (iii) PSF+S\u00e9rsic. We found that a single PSF model left significant residuals in the <em>i</em>-band image, as expected, and a PSF+S\u00e9rsic model was not an improvement over the single S\u00e9rsic model. Therefore, our final best model for the lensed source is a single S\u00e9rsic component.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We run Monte Carlo Markov Chain (MCMC) using <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span> (Foreman-Mackey et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). The model fitting includes pixels within a 7\u2009arcsec box centred on the lens (see the left-hand panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>). The medians of the posterior distributions of our model parameters are given in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. We only report the source magnitude and effective radius from our model in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> since other source parameters such as the ellipticity and position angle (PA) are not well constrained. Also, since the S\u00e9rsic index <em>n</em> was not well constrained, we fixed it to <em>n</em> = 4 that corresponds to the de Vaucoleurs\u2019 profile.</p>                        <a id=\"48563946\" scrollto-destination=\"48563946\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 6.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/465/2/10.1093_mnras_stw2924/3/m_stw2924fig6.jpeg?Expires=1611466900&amp;Signature=E1EzAd~k0D31H0htLSev3UhQ6NQRV22-EN8zHmxEJn-AL6r2~M4nhmKfQSQMIaI1mMh-jlh3OpOXbA0GX--W5nhEHsJSxchuD0lGhM6szEwRAujjXdRG4lXjjd79fWGP3V5wT3wxTdtytO9Gmilpw5hKxOe3kE~a2wy-AyZg7P86tbN9G9xPduLwwqVSoCpOFSRffP6V1831Y0kOj6TtH6hQ3Oi7ozof82~2WVhwr53jtWHtmWrgJagQ9HpjDJef81lKdxXk5HmBLPi0sX0GP8e3fNgvls9cAgGc-rGL~~p8Bm3eA18YJ03ZAu-iqH0p~4NYbmNbi95kI6VTBDiiPg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"HSC i-band image ( left), lens mass model where the background source is assumed to have a S\u00e9rsic profile (middle) and the residual image (right). class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/48563947\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, we show the model image (middle panel) and the residual image (right-hand panel) after subtracting the model from the data. We also show critical curves (white contours) and caustics (magenta contours) marking the regions of infinite magnification in the middle panel. We highlight the peak positions of the model-predicted lensed images (circles) and the true source position (triangle).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Finally, we also constructed mass models by fitting to just the peak positions and relative fluxes of the lensed images which were measured with <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>. We tested similar lens models as before except that the source is assumed to be a point source since the data constraints are limited. From this analysis, we determined a magnification factor of \u03bc = 2.5\u20133 for images A and B. We choose \u03bc = 2.5<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a> for the analysis presented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563950 id=\"48563950\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we determine the properties of the lensed source based on the modelling of the imaging and spectroscopic data. First, we analyse the single most prominent emission line found in the spectra of the lensed images. Next, we determine the true source magnitude and size to decide whether the source is dominated by an AGN or not.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=48563952 id=\"48563952\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-1>5.1 Ly-\u03b1 emission</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We fit a Gaussian to the one-dimensional spectrum around the Ly-\u03b1 emission in images A and B (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a>). We obtain a line width of \u03c3 = 4.6 \u00b1 0.3\u2009\u00c5 and 5.4 \u00b1 0.4\u00a0\u00c5 at 5788\u00a0\u00c5, respectively. Given the spectral resolution (<em>R</em> = 1918), the instrumental broadening corresponds to 156\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>. Thus, the full width at half-Maximum (FWHM) of Ly-\u03b1 emission from images A and B, after accounting for the instrumental broadening, is 540\u00a0\u00b1\u00a040 and 640\u00a0\u00b1\u00a050\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively. There is a second peak blueward of the Ly-\u03b1 line, which suggests that the underlying Ly\u00a0\u03b1 is much broader, with different parts of the emission line modified by absorption or resonant scattering from surrounding neutral hydrogen (e.g. Miralda-Escud\u00e9\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1998</a>). This absorption feature is more obvious for image A than for image B. The FWHMs of the broader Ly-\u03b1 line (including the second peak to the left) of images A and B are \u223c920 and \u223c890\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively. This is also consistent with the FWHM estimate measured from the stacked (unbinned) spectra of the four images shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"48563954\" .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>16 \u00b1 1 (30)\u00a0</td><td>540 \u00b1 40 (920)\u00a0</td><td>3.2 (6.3)\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>15 \u00b1 1 (26)\u00a0</td><td>640 \u00b1 50 (890)\u00a0</td><td>3.2 (6.3)\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th>Name<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>EW<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>FWHM<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>L</em> \u00d7 10<sup>42</sup><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u00c5<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>km s<sup>\u22121</sup><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>erg s<sup>\u22121</sup><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>16 \u00b1 1 (30)\u00a0</td><td>540 \u00b1 40 (920)\u00a0</td><td>3.2 (6.3)\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>15 \u00b1 1 (26)\u00a0</td><td>640 \u00b1 50 (890)\u00a0</td><td>3.2 (6.3)\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-wrap-foot\"><span id=\"fn-TFN002\"></span><div content-id=\"TFN002\"><span class=\"fn\"><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Notes.</em> The values are given for the narrow component of Ly \u03b1 and the values within parentheses are given for the broader component. The EW is given in the rest frame. The luminosities are delensed by taking into account the magnification factor.</p></span></div></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/48563956\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=48563957 id=\"48563957\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-2>5.2 Source magnitude</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\"><span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model fitting to image A in the <em>i</em>-band suggested <em>i</em> = 22.82. Using the magnification factor of \u03bc = 2.5, we obtain a delensed source magnitude of <em>i</em> = 23.84. At <em>z</em><sub>s</sub> = 3.76, this corresponds to an absolute magnitude <em>M</em><sub>1500</sub> = \u221222.1 after applying a <em>K</em>-correction for the continuum <em>K</em><sub>corr</sub> = \u22122.5(1 + \u03b1) log (1 + <em>z</em><sub>s</sub>) \u2212 2.5\u2009\u03b1 log (1500\u2009\u00c5/\u03bb<sub>eff</sub>) following the prescription of Glikman et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>), where \u03b1 = \u22120.5 (Richards et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>) for quasars.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Alternatively, we can determine the true source magnitude from the results of our lens mass modelling by fitting an extended source to the <em>i</em>-band image. The best-fitting source magnitude thus obtained is <em>i</em> = 23.9 \u00b1 0.1, which corresponds to <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> = \u221222.0 \u00b1 0.1.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=48563960 id=\"48563960\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-3>5.3 Source size</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We followed the same two approaches as before to estimate the true source size. <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model fitting suggested a size of <em>r</em><sub>eff, l</sub> = 0.12\u00a0arcsec, where subscript <em>l</em> indicates lensed. The lensing magnification factor (\u03bc = 2.5) is used to scale down the area of the lensed source to get the true source size. We assume the source is circular (i.e. area = <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\pi r_{\\rm {eff,t}}^2$|</span> where subscript <em>t</em> implies true) and that the lensing magnification is the same in all directions. Thus, the true source size is estimated to be <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$r_{\\rm {eff,t}}=\\scriptstyle\\sqrt{r_{\\rm {eff,l}}^2/\\mu }= 0.069$|</span>\u00a0arcsec. Given the physical scale at <em>z</em><sub>s</sub> = 3.76 of 7.33\u00a0kpc arcsec<sup>\u22121</sup>, the source size is \u223c0.56\u00a0kpc in physical units.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The best-fitting source size <em>r</em><sub>eff, t</sub> from lens mass modelling is found to be even smaller, \u223c0.028 \u00b1 0.005\u00a0arcsec, suggesting a physical source size of 0.20 \u00b1 0.04\u00a0kpc. We trust our latter estimate more because the former estimate has a larger systematic error owing to the strong assumptions adopted in our calculation.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563963 id=\"48563963\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>6 DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we compare properties of high-redshift galaxies with the lensed source of HSC J1152+0047 to decide whether the source has an AGN or not. We also discuss possible sources of chromatic variations seen in the flux ratio of the brighter pair of lensed images.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=48563965 id=\"48563965\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6-1>6.1 Comparison with high-redshift galaxies</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Here, we consider comparison with LAEs and LBGs. LAEs are SFGs with faint ultraviolet (UV) continuum and a prominent Ly-\u03b1 emission line (e.g. Malhotra &amp; Rhoads\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>; Ouchi et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Shibuya et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib47\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib47\" data-open=\"bib47\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">LAEs are known to have large EW<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$_{{\\rm Ly\\text{-}\\alpha }}$|</span> and small velocity widths <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\Delta v_{{\\rm Ly\\text{-}\\alpha }}$|</span> (e.g. Ouchi et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Hashimoto et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). For example, rest-frame EW<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$_{{\\rm Ly\\text{-}\\alpha }}$|</span> for a sample of LAEs at <em>z</em> \u223c 3.7 is &gt;44 \u00c5 (Ouchi et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) much larger than that of our lensed source but their sample is limited by the selection effects of their narrow-band data. Usually LAEs are defined to be high-redshift galaxies with rest-frame EW<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$_{{\\rm Ly\\text{-}\\alpha }}\\ge 20\\,$|</span>\u00c5 (e.g. Ouchi et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). Thus, our source is probably at the border of being an LAE based on the EW criteria. Ouchi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) measured velocity widths of Ly\u00a0\u03b1 for a large sample of over 200 LAE at high redshifts. They find average velocity widths of 260\u00a0km\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> without significant difference between <em>z</em> = 5.7 and 6.6. Assuming that this holds true at <em>z</em> \u223c 4, the FWHM of Ly\u00a0\u03b1 for the source in HSC J1152+0047 is much broader to be an LAE type of galaxy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The characteristic of LBGs is a sharp drop in the flux bluewards of the Ly\u00a0\u03b1. We do not see any strong evidence of such a sharp cut-off in either the spectrum (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>) or the imaging although its colours may not be inconsistent with an LBG (e.g. Bouwens et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Shibuya, Ouchi &amp; Harikane (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) derived the size\u2013magnitude (<em>M</em><sub>UV</sub>) relation for SFGs and LBGs at <em>z</em> \u223c 4 (as shown in fig. 9 of their paper). Our source is an outlier for the <em>z</em> \u223c 4 population and does not show expected size and magnitude properties for either SFGs or LBGs at that redshift. In fact, it is extremely compact and at the brighter end of the rest of the population suggesting that the source probably has an AGN with low-to-moderate luminosity.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=48563970 id=\"48563970\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6-2>6.2 Chromatic variations in the flux ratios</h3>\r\n                    <h4 scrollto-destination=48563971 id=\"48563971\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6-2-1>6.2.1 Intrinsic variability due to AGN</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">AGNs are known to show intrinsic variability over months to years whereas galaxies do not have any mechanism to produce variability on such time-scales, except when they host luminous transients such as supernovae. From the geometrical symmetry of the lensed images in an <em>Einstein</em> cross and from our mass models, the relative time delay in the arrival of light rays between the brighter pair of the lensed images is a few days. The difference of 1 mag in the brighter pair (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>), which are separated by a relative time delay of a few days, is too rapid to be caused by intrinsic flux variation in the AGN.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=48563973 id=\"48563973\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6-2-2>6.2.2 Microlensing</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Microlensing is the lensing effect produced by very low mass compact components, for example, from stars, globular clusters or black holes in the plane of a lens galaxy. Microlensing could produce chromatic variations in flux ratios. This effect is sensitive to the scale of the source size. The size of region emitting rest-frame optical (observed frame NIR) in the source plane is much larger than that of the region emitting rest-frame UV (observed frame optical). Thus, the <em>Ks</em>-band fluxes are expected to be less affected by microlensing. However, HSC J1152+0047 shows a discrepancy in the <em>Ks</em>-band flux ratio instead of the optical.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Also, the VIKING <em>Z</em> band (not shown here) suggests a flux ratio, B/A\u223c1 consistent with HSC data. The VIKING <em>Ks</em>-band data were taken 1\u00a0yr after VIKING <em>Z</em>-band data and HSC data were taken 4\u00a0yr after <em>Ks</em>-band data. Ignoring the fact that we are comparing data in different bands here, these time-scales, even though short, are still feasible for microlensing to appear, based on the caustic crossing time estimated by following Treyer &amp; Wambsganss (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Microlensing can also be deduced by comparing the continuum and emission line flux ratios (e.g. Sluse et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>). The ratio of the flux-calibrated optical spectra of images A and B is nearly 1, for both the continuum and the Ly-\u03b1 emission line disfavouring the microlensing scenario although the weaker NV and C\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> emission lines have different relative strengths but are not reliable. In summary, microlensing cannot be considered as the source of the discrepancy between the optical and NIR flux ratios without additional data. High-resolution and deeper spectroscopy is needed to rule out or confirm microlensing with certainty.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=48563977 id=\"48563977\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6-2-3>6.2.3 Differential extinction</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Extinction from the presence of dust in the lens galaxy could affect the flux ratios as a function of the wavelength. First, the lens galaxy is an early-type galaxy which usually does not have significant dust to cause severe extinction of the lensed images (e.g. Falco et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1999</a>; El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). Secondly, we do not see any obvious dust lanes in the deep HSC imaging. Given that the system is fairly symmetrical in the near north\u2013south direction and that images A and B are almost equidistant on the either side of the lens galaxy, the amount of dust is not expected to be significantly different to cause such a high level of differential extinction in the lensed images A and B and in the <em>Ks</em> band alone. Also, extinction affects strongly at shorter wavelengths but the flux ratio B/A does not show any sign of extinction in the HSC data. Furthermore, the measured flux ratios in the optical are completely consistent with a simple lens mass model without the need to include any model for the dust extinction. Thus, extinction is unlikely to be the source of chromatic variation seen in HSC J1152+0047.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563979 id=\"48563979\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec7>7 CONCLUSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have discovered a quadruply imaged source from the HSC survey at <em>z</em><sub>s</sub> = 3.76 lensed by an early-type galaxy at <em>z</em><sub>l</sub> = 0.466. In this paper, we attempt to understand whether the background source has an AGN at its core by considering various observational evidences. <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">The Ly-\u03b1 emission line is narrow but broader than velocity widths typically found in LAEs and LBGs and its luminosity is consistent with a faint AGN. The rest-frame UV spectrum shows weak metal emission lines (NV and blue-wing absorbed C\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span>) found in the narrow-line region/broad-line region around AGNs.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">The rest-frame UV absolute magnitude (<em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> = \u221222) of the source suggests that it belongs to either the rare population of intrinsically bright galaxy population (e.g. Kobayashi, Totani &amp; Nagashima\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Parsa et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) or the very abundant population of faint AGNs (e.g. Glikman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">The source appears point-like in all bands except <em>i</em> band where only the brighter pair of lensed images suggest extendedness in the tangential direction. This could suggest that we are detecting emission from the underlying AGN host galaxy. Alternatively, if there is no AGN, then the source (with <em>r</em><sub>eff</sub> \u223c 0.2\u00a0kpc) is one of the most compact galaxies at <em>z</em> \u223c 4.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">We do not see any sign of variability in any of the four lensed images from the HSC data and VIKING <em>Z</em> and <em>Y</em> bands except the VIKING <em>Ks</em>-band data which show highly discrepant flux ratio for the brighter pair. The origin of the chromatic variation is not understood since none of the known phenomena such as intrinsic variability, microlensing and differential extinction can fully explain the measured optical and NIR flux ratios. We cannot rule out microlensing due to lack of sufficient data.</p></li></ul></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In conclusion, the lensed source is most probably a low-luminosity AGN (LLAGN) but we cannot exclude the possibility of it being a rare, highly compact bright galaxy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">If the source indeed has an AGN, then this represents the highest redshift quadruply lensed AGN. Conventionally, quasars (or bright AGNs) are favourable for cosmological studies as they are easier to follow-up on small telescopes for time-delay monitoring. However, LLAGNs also show variability, in fact, at much shorter time-scales and the high redshift, quadruply lensed source of HSC J1152+0047 shows great promise for cosmological studies. Faint AGNs are also useful to study quasar fuelling lifetime, feedback and faint end slope of the luminosity function (e.g. Hopkins et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Glikman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Fiore et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Ikeda et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Matsuoka et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). Studying LLAGNs at high redshifts has been difficult due to their faintness and strong lensing might be the best means to study inner structures and mechanisms that drive the nuclear activity in distant LLAGNs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Most of the surveys in the past have been shallow and wide enough to detect numerous lensed quasars (the brighter end of the AGN luminosity function). The discovery of HSC J1152+0047 from early HSC data demonstrates that HSC, with its unique combination of depth and wide imaging, is beginning to probe the fainter end of the AGN luminosity function. Looking ahead, we should expect to discover more lensed LLAGNs from HSC which will open a new avenue of studies. In the future, with even larger surveys such as the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope survey, lensed LLAGNs will probably be discovered routinely through variability.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=48563984 id=\"48563984\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgments</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">AM is supported by World Premier International Research Center Initiative (WPI Initiative), MEXT, Japan. "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017MNRAS.465.2411M", "Tables status": "Complete", "Inspection status": "Incomplete", "Paper Overview": {"abstract": "We report the serendipitous discovery of a quadruply lensed source at z<SUB>s</SUB> = 3.76, HSC J115252+004733, from the Hyper Suprime-Cam (HSC) Survey. The source is lensed by an early-type galaxy at z<SUB>l</SUB> = 0.466 and a satellite galaxy. Here, we investigate the properties of the source by studying its size and luminosity from the imaging and the luminosity and velocity width of the Ly-\u03b1 line from the spectrum. Our analyses suggest that the source is most probably a low-luminosity active galactic nucleus (LLAGN) but the possibility of it being a compact bright galaxy (e.g. a Lyman-\u03b1 emitter or lyman break galaxy) cannot be excluded. The brighter pair of lensed images appears point-like except in the HSC I band (with a seeing \u223c0.5 arcsec). The extended emission in the I-band image could be due to the host galaxy underneath the AGN, or alternatively, due to a highly compact lensed galaxy (without AGN) which appears point-like in all bands except in I band. We also find that the flux ratio of the brighter pair of images is different in the Ks band compared to optical wavelengths. Phenomena such as differential extinction and intrinsic variability cannot explain this chromatic variation. While microlensing from stars in the foreground galaxy is less likely to be the cause, it cannot be ruled out completely. If the galaxy hosts an AGN, then this represents the highest redshift quadruply imaged AGN known to date, enabling study of a distant LLAGN. Discovery of this unusually compact and faint source demonstrates the potential of the HSC survey.", "start_page": "2411", "authors": ["More, Anupreeta", "Lee, Chien-Hsiu", "Oguri, Masamune", "Ono, Yoshiaki", "Suyu, Sherry H.", "Chan, James H.
